Preface
The work presented in this report covers the main outcome of my master thesis attached to my civil
engineering degree at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU). The work was conducted during
the period: 03.08-2015 - 29.12-2015. The primary supervision was given by Professor Ole Sigmund
and Associate Professor Niels Aage from the Department of Mechanical Engineering at DTU. The
report including an oral defence corresponds to a total work load of 30 ECTS points equivalent to one
semester of study.
Acknowledgements

mund and Lektor Niels Aage at DTU whom has o’¼Ćered excellent supervision and contributed to a
productive and pleasant study environment. Without their encouragement in the project, ability to
bring up relevant and interesting discussions, and rapid responses to e-mails, the ’¼ünal thesis would
never have reached this stage.
In addition, I would like to thank Susana Rojas Labanda (Ph.D. student at DTU) for her guidance
related to setting up and using ipOPT.
Furthermore I would like to give a special recognition to Mads J. Baandrup for his willingness to share
his work and Matlab scripts upon which the growth-method presented in this thesis is based. MadsŌĆÖ
scripts included a main 2D growth-method program, and volume and compliance functions used for
the objective/constraint functions. Furthermore various plotting routines were implemented. With
MadsŌĆÖ permission these scripts were modi’¼üed and extended in order to optimize convergence, optimize
run-time, implement multiple load cases, add geometric constraints, include 3D structures, implement
an eigenvalue constraint, use beam elements and various other modi’¼ücations.
Finally, I would like to express my gratitude to Daniel Fester Henningsen for proofreading and cor-
recting the report.
ii
==PAGE:4==
Abstract
Optimization of truss structures, be it topology optimization, shape optimization, or a combination
of both, is usually performed using a ground structure approach. The ground structure approach is
known to require a very ’¼üne mesh in order to produce reasonable results. In this thesis an alternative
optimization method is described and analysed; the growth-method. This method is studied in both
two and three dimensions. The method is based on the work performed in [10] and [2].
The growth-method does not require a ground structure, only the location of the bounded nodes and
the loaded nodes. The growth algorithm will then add new truss members in a systematic manner,
always ensuring that the objective function (the volume of the truss structure in this thesis) is reduced
and the compliance constraint obeyed in each growth step or iteration. In 3D it is made certain that
the optimized structures are stable through either eigenvalue constraints or the use of beam elements.
Furthermore the user can enable geometric constraints, which are areas of the design domain in which
no members are allowed to exist. This feature is not implemented for 3D though. The main optimizer
used is fmincon in Matlab, but ipOPT is also investigated.
Results obtained when optimizing simple structures using the growth-method, e.g. a cantilever, have
been found to resemble optimal Michell structures.
When veri’¼üed against analytical least-weight
ŌĆØMichell-cantileversŌĆØ in [15] an almost perfect match was found. The growth-methods ability to han-
dle multiple load cases is presented through various examples in both 2D and 3D. In order to verify
the 3D version of the method the classical Michell torsion-ball example is studied, and it is found
that the growth-method is able to produce the well known spherical structure. Lastly, applying the
method to more practical applications is presented through the use of geometric constraints.
Keywords: Truss optimization, 2D, 3D, growth-method, multiple load cases, eigenvalue constraint,
beam elements, geometric constraint, Matlab, fmincon, ipOPT.

Sammendrag
Optimering af gitterstrukturer, hvad enten det er topologioptimering, formoptimering eller en kombi-
nation af begge, udf├Ėres s├”dvanligvis ved anvendelse af en grundstrukturmetode. Denne grundstruktur
fremgangsm╦Üade er kendt for at kr├”ve en meget ’¼ün opdeling for at kunne fremstille rimelige resultater.
I denne afhandling er en alternativ optimeringsmetode beskrevet og analyseret; ŌĆØthe growth-methodŌĆØ.
Denne metode er udforsket i b╦Üade to og tre dimensioner. Metoden er baseret p╦Üa det udf├Ėrte arbejde
i [10] og [2].
ŌĆØThe growth-methodŌĆØ kr├”ver ikke en grundstruktur, kun placeringen af de underst├Ėttede knuder
og de lastede knuder. ŌĆØGrowthŌĆØ-algoritmen tilf├Ėjer nye gitterelementer p╦Üa en systematisk m╦Üade, og
sikrer altid at objekt funktionen (volumen af gitterkonstruktionen i denne afhandling) reduceres og at
ŌĆØcomplianceŌĆØ-begr├”nsningen overholdes i hver v├”kst eller iteration. I 3D s├Ėrger enten begr├”nsninger
p╦Üa egenv├”rdier eller brugen af bj├”lke elementer for, at de optimerede konstruktioner er stabile. Desu-
den kan brugeren aktivere geometriske begr├”nsninger, som er omr╦Üader af design omr╦Üadet, hvor ingen
elementer m╦Üa eksistere. Disse begr├”nsninger er ikke implementeret i 3D modellen. Den anvendte
optimeringsalgoritme er fmincon i Matlab, men ipOPT er ogs╦Üa unders├Ėgt kort.
Resultater opn╦Üaet ved optimering af simple konstruktioner under anvendelse af ŌĆØgrowth-methodŌĆØ,
f.eks en indsp├”ndt bj├”lke, har vist sig at ligne optimale Michell strukturer. N╦Üar resultatet veri’¼üceres
mod en analytisk ŌĆØMichell-cantileverŌĆØ i [15] ’¼ündes en n├”sten perfekt sammenh├”ng. Metodens evne til
at h╦Üandtere multiple last tilf├”lde er illustreret i forskellige eksempler i b╦Üade 2D og 3D. Som veri’¼ücering
af metoden i 3D er der kigget p╦Üa den klassiske Michell ŌĆØtorsion-ballŌĆØ, og det er vist at metoden kan
fremstille den velkendte kugleform. Til sidst fokuseres der p╦Üa metodens mere praktisk orienterede
egenskaber ved hj├”lp af de geometriske begr├”nsninger.
N├Ėgleord: Gitteroptimering, 2D, 3D, ŌĆØgrowth-methodŌĆØ, multiple lasttilf├”lde, egenv├”rdi begr├”n-
sning, bj├”lke elementer, geometriske begr├”nsninger, Matlab, fmincon, ipOPT.

Chapter 1
Introduction
1.1
Background
This master thesis is written on the presumption that the reader has basic knowledge about structural
optimization, especially truss optimization, and general optimization algorithms. The authorŌĆÖs expe-
rience in structural optimization is not vast, as only introductory courses have been attended. For
this particular reason, the theory chapter of this master thesis does include some very basic theory
on truss topology and shape optimization before focusing on the actual subject of the thesis, i.e. the
growth-method.
1.2
Motivation
Whereas standard truss optimization requires a ground structure, which has to be extremely ’¼üne
if proper results are desired, the growth method introduced by [11] is initialized by de’¼üning the
boundary nodes and the loaded nodes and connecting these with bars. In Figure 1.1 below the ground
structure approach (using di’¼Ćerent levels of ’¼üneness) and the growth-method are illustrated for a
simple cantilever example.
a Ground structure approach (top and bottom
left corner nodes are supported, and middle right
edge node is loaded). Taken from [18].
b Growth-method.
Figure 1.1: Ground structure approach vs. Growth-method.
As seen from the ’¼ügure the ground structure approach contains a large number of bars in the ’¼üne
meshes and might also require even more nodes than shown in the ’¼ügure. After the optimization dead
1
==PAGE:20==
1.3.
Challenges
2
elements (close to zero cross-sectional area) can be removed, and the ’¼ünal optimized structure can be
visualized. The growth-method, on the other hand, contains only 3 nodes and two bars in the initial
structure. The growth algorithm then adds new nodes and bars in an optimal manner such that the
objective function is always lowered from growth to growth. The growth method is advantageous when
it comes to controlling the complexity of the optimized structure. In the ground structure approach
it is hard to determine what kind of mesh is required to obtain a certain amount of nodes in the ’¼ünal
structure, whereas in the growth-method the complexity of the ’¼ünal structure is easily managed by
specifying how many growths are desired, since exactly one node is added in each growth.
In this thesis it is attempted to generalize the growth-method presented in [10], where the optimiza-
tion is based on a shape optimization in which the cross-sectional areas of the bars are determined
from the allowable stresses. This method is only valid for statically determinant structures, and thus
features such as multiple load cases cannot be implemented. By introducing a combined simultaneous
topology- and shape optimization while keeping to the way of growing the structure as presented in
[10] the growth-method will be able to handle features such as multiple load cases and additional
constraints.
Furthermore it is attempted to expand the growth-method to three-dimensional truss structures,
introduce eigenvalue constraints and the use of beam elements to maintain stability, and implement
geometric constraints, i.e. introduce areas in the design domain in which bars are not allowed to exist.
1.3
Challenges
The main challenge when developing a numerical optimization method such as the growth-method
for truss structures is to ensure that the algorithm is robust and complete enough to handle various
types of problems. For example in the 2D growth-method the structures can become unstable in
some of the iterations without violating the compliance constraint. It is therefore important that the
algorithm recognizes when this happens, so that it does not misinterpret the unstable structure as
an optimal one. Another example is that the algorithm should be able to realize when nodes want
to merge. Restrictions on the nodal movement during shape optimization prevents nodes from ever
touching each other. Therefore the shape part of the optimization cannot by itself merge nodes. An
extra check is therefore implemented in order to merge nodes when needed. In the 3D growth method
stability problems is the main challenge. Three dimensional truss structures very easily become un-
stable, and therefore two features are implemented to keep the structure stable; either a constraint on
the structures eigenvalues can be used or beam elements (which are stable by themselves) can be used.
When dealing with algorithms another main challenge is to reduce the run-time of the program. Spend-
ing su’¼ācient time on simple code optimization can generally decrease the run-time considerably, but
improving the general structure of the growth-method can vastly reduce the run-time. Improving the
general structure of the growth-method could for example consist of implementing restrictions on how
to add new members, such as the crossing restriction and the indeterminacy restriction described in
the report.
Furthermore it is challenging to make the growth-method useful for practical applications. One mea-
sure taken to overcome this challenge is to implement geometric constraints. In practical applications
geometric constraints are often needed due to the crossing of various installations such as ventilation.
Louis Christo’¼Ćersen (s102928)
==PAGE:21==
1.4.
Objective
3
1.4
Objective
The goals of this study are listed below:
1. Generalize the growth-method presented in [10] in order to include features which
cannot be included in a statically determinant method.
i This involves implementing a combined simultaneous topology and shape optimization in-
stead of simply a shape optimization in which the cross-sectional areas are determined from
the allowable stresses.
2. Implement the possibility to run multiple load cases.
i This is possible due to the generalization described above.
3. Compare results to analytical ŌĆØMichell-structuresŌĆØ.
4. Expand the growth-method to create optimal three-dimensional truss structures.
i Implement the possibility to constrain eigenvalues of the structure.
ii Include the possibility of using beam elements instead of truss elements to ensure stability
of the optimized structure.
5. Introduce geometric constraints, i.e. introduce areas in the design domain in which
bars are not allowed to exist.
i This could for example be a circular constraint.
1.5
Outline of Thesis
The main report will consist of the following chapters:
┬ł Chapter 2 - Theory
ŌĆō This chapter describes the theory behind topology and shape optimization, along with com-
bined topology and shape optimization in both 2D and 3D. Furthermore it introduces the
growth-method as described in [10] and explains the theory behind the di’¼Ćerent extensions
and features implemented by the author.
┬ł Chapter 3 - Results & Discussion
ŌĆō This chapter presents results of truss structures optimized using the growth-method. A
variety of examples demonstrate di’¼Ćerent key features of the growth-method in both two
and three dimensions. All results are discussed thoroughly in this chapter as well.
┬ł Chapter 4 - Conclusion and further work
As a supplement to the main report an appendix report has been written.
The appendix report
consists of every Matlab script used throughout the thesis, various mathematical derivations, and
additional results which are not included in chapter 3.
The appendix report is attached as an additional report after the bibliography.

Chapter 2
Theory
The purpose of this chapter is to provide an overview of the various optimization methods utilized in
this master thesis. These methods are obviously described for truss structures. First, basic topology
and shape optimization methods are described in two dimensions. A combined topology and shape
optimization technique is also described. Furthermore the basic idea and algorithm used in the growth-
method presented in [10] is explained. Lastly extensions and additional features (implemented by the
Author), e.g. the growth-method in three dimensions, eigenvalue constraints, and geometric constraints
are set forth.
2.1
Structural optimization (trusses)
The two basic optimization methods utilized in this master thesis are topology optimization and shape
optimization. They can be de’¼üned as follows:
De’¼ünition 1. Topology optimization: The objective function is minimized by distributing the al-
lowable volume of material in the most optimal manner, i.e. by changing truss-member cross-sectional
areas.
De’¼ünition 2. Shape optimization: The objective function is minimized by moving the nodes of the
truss structure to the most optimal positions.
This means that the design variables (see De’¼ünition 3) are the cross-sectional areas, A, when perform-
ing topology optimization, and the nodal coordinates, x and y, when performing shape optimization.
De’¼ünition 3. Design variables: The speci’¼üc variables which are strategically changed in each
iteration of an optimization process in order to improve the value of the objective function, i.e. lower
the value.
Both optimization methods utilize one of two di’¼Ćerent objective functions (see De’¼ünition 4). Either
the compliance, which is simply the displacement of the loaded degree of freedom (dof) multiplied by
the applied force, or the volume of the truss structure is minimized.
De’¼ünition 4. Objective function: The goal of the optimization process which is to be minimized
by iteratively changing the design variables.
Most optimization problems need one or more constraints (see De’¼ünition 5) in order to produce rea-
sonable solutions. This is also the case for truss optimization. If the objective function is compliance,
the volume needs to be constrained, and if the objective function is volume, the compliance should be
constrained.
5
==PAGE:24==
2.2.
Topology optimization
6
De’¼ünition 5. Constraint function: Can either be an equality or inequality which has to be ful’¼ülled
in any optimal solution.
The restrictiveness of the constraints is usually set by a factor which is multiplied by the initial value of
either the volume or compliance (depending on which is constrained) of the truss structure. Usually
lower and upper bound values are enforced on the design variables in order to obtain reasonable
solutions. In topology optimization of truss structures for example, the cross-sectional areas have a
lower bound value of a very small number (usually of the order of magnitude of 10ŌłÆ6) in order to
remove low density bars in a post-processing step. It also ensures that all cross-sectional areas stay
positive and hence that the global sti’¼Ćness matrix remains positive de’¼ünite. In shape optimization the
nodal coordinates are usually bounded within some local domain in order to not change the layout of
the truss structure too drastically.
2.2
Topology optimization
A typical truss topology optimization problem with the compliance as objective function, f, and the
volume as the constraint function, g, can be formulated as

2.2.2
Examples
To demonstrate the use of topology optimization, results for a simple example are presented in this
section. The Matlab script used for topology optimization is called TopOpt.m (see Appendix H.1).
Depending on whether compliance is the objective function and volume the constraint function or
vice versa, this script calls either dispfunc.m or volumefunc.m (see Appendix H.2 and H.3) as the
objective function and either volumecon.m or dispcon.m (see Appendix H.4 and H.5) as the constraint
function. A standard input-’¼üle providing the nodal coordinates, the connectivity, and the load and
boundary conditions can be seen in Appendix H.6. The initial structure along with the optimized
structure can be seen in Figure 2.2 below. Both the initial and optimized structures are plotted by
the truss.m script (see Appendix H.7).
a Initial ground structure (all members have equal area). b Optimized structure (blue is tension and red is com-
pression).
c Optimized deformed structure.
Figure 2.2: Topology optimization of cantilever beam with compliance as the objective function and the volume
constrained.
In this example the compliance is minimized and the constraint on the volume is 1 times the initial
volume.
The optimizer redistributes the cross-sectional areas making the top and bottom chords
thicker as this is where the largest stresses occur. Notice that the line thickness in the plots represents
cross-sectional area (thicker lines indicates larger cross-sectional areas). Additionally it is observed
that the cross-sectional area is largest closest to the support. This makes sense considering that the
largest moment occurs at the support and therefore also the largest stress must occur here.
The
stresses in the truss structure are calculated by the Matlab-function stressfunc.m (see Appendix
H.8). The performance of the optimization can be assessed by
Reduction = finitial ŌłÆfoptimized
finitial
├Ś 100%
(2.8)
The initial compliance was reduced by 76.2 % in this example.
2.3
Shape optimization
Shape optimization is another typical method of optimizing a truss structure. The nodal coordinates
are now considered as the design variables and the cross-sectional area is set to some constant value.
Shape optimization can naturally also consider either the compliance or the volume as the objective
Louis Christo’¼Ćersen (s102928)
==PAGE:27==
2.3.
Shape optimization
9

and nn is the number of total nodes (multiplied by 2 since there are two nodal coordinates per node).
In most cases one would set the lower and upper values to be within the domain of the truss structure,
i.e. the space de’¼üned by the outermost lying nodes. In certain truss structures it may be an advantage
or maybe even a necessity to let the lower and upper bound values exceed the domain. It should be
noted that the nodal coordinates of the supported and loaded nodes are not a part of the design
variables.

2.3.2
Examples
To demonstrate the use of shape optimization, results of a simple example are presented in this section.
The Matlab script used for shape optimization is called GeomOpt.m (see Appendix H.9). Depending
on whether compliance is the objective function and volume the constraint function or vice versa, this
script calls the same four functions as the topology optimization script. The optimized structures can
be seen in Figure 2.3 below. Notice that the cantilever beam is optimized for both compliance and
volume, Figures 2.3a and 2.3b respectively. The initial structure is not shown, as it is identical to the
one used for the topology optimization example (see Figure 2.2a).
a Optimized structure, object compliance and constraint
1.1 x initial volume.
b Optimized structure, object volume and constraint 1.4
x initial compliance.
Figure 2.3: Shape optimization of cantilever beam (blue is tension, red is compression, and green is zero stress).
The optimizer moves the free nodes (all except the boundary and loaded nodes) to create the most
optimal structure. It is observed that when the compliance is the objective function the elements are
likely to be longer (increases the volume of the structure) compared to when the volume is the objective
to minimize. Notice that the structure optimized for compliance is not completely symmetrical like
the one optimized for volume. Also notice how some nodes in the structure optimized for compliance
have moved past each other and therefore causes some of the bars to cross.
As for the topology
optimization the performance of the shape optimization can be assessed using Equation 2.8. In the
compliance minimization the initial compliance was reduced by 50.3 %, and in the volume minimization
the initial volume was reduced by 35.9 %.
Louis Christo’¼Ćersen (s102928)
==PAGE:29==
2.4.
Combined topology and shape optimization
11
2.4
Combined topology and shape optimization
To obtain even better optimized truss structures both optimization methods described above can be
combined. Instead of only optimizing the topology or the shape of the truss structure one at a time
it can be advantageous to optimize both features at once. This is relatively easy to implement for
truss structures. There are di’¼Ćerent methods of how to combine topology and shape optimization, but
in this work only the simultaneous method will be discussed. Simultaneous optimization can handle
both the compliance or the volume as the objective function, and the other as the constraint. The
Matlab script used for combined topology and shape optimization is called TGopt.m (see Appendix
H.10). The objective functions for the simultaneous method are called TGfunc.m and TGvolumefunc.m
(see Appendix H.11 and H.12) for the compliance and volume respectively. The constraint functions
are called TGcondisp.m and TGcon.m (see Appendix H.13 and H.14) for the compliance and volume
respectively.
2.4.1
Simultaneous method
This method combines the topology optimization and shape optimization by increasing the amount of
design variables. The design variables are now both the cross-sectional areas (topology optimization)
and the nodal coordinates (shape optimization). The lower and upper bound vectors, and the sen-
sitivity vectors for both the areas and the nodal coordinates should therefore also be assembled into
one vector.
Example
To illustrate the simultaneous method the same cantilever is used once more. The optimized structure
with compliance as the objective can be seen in Figure 2.4 below. In this example the constraint on
the volume is 1.1 x initial volume.
Figure 2.4: Combined optimization (simultaneous method) of cantilever beam with compliance as objective
(blue is tension, and red is compression).
It is observed how the optimizer has made changes to both the cross-sectional areas and the nodal
coordinates as expected. The initial compliance was reduced by 82.3 %, calculated using Equation 2.8.
It can clearly be seen how this optimized structure is a combination of a topology optimization and a
shape optimization. The outer bars have the larger cross-sectional areas precisely as in the topology
optimized structure in Figure 2.2b, and the rounded shape resembles the shape optimized structure
in Figure 2.3a.
2.5
Growth-method
The growth method is fundamentally di’¼Ćerent from the other optimization methods discussed so far.
Whereas topology and shape optimization requires an initial ground structure, which for topology
Louis Christo’¼Ćersen (s102928)
==PAGE:30==
2.5.
Growth-method
12
optimization needs to be fairly densely meshed in order to obtain reasonable results, the growth
method requires nothing but the de’¼ünition of the bounded and loaded nodes. A certain design domain
is speci’¼üed and the bounded and loaded nodes are connected by initial truss elements (usually not
more than a few bars depending on the problem). A topology optimization is carried out on this
initial structure to determine which initial bars are required. The topology and shape optimization
used throughout the growth-method is a combined topology and shape optimization utilizing the
simultaneous method (see subsection 2.4.1). The growth-algorithm then adds new nodes and bars
following certain rules and criteria, which are described later in this section, to create an optimized
truss structure within the initially de’¼üned design domain. The growth-algorithm used in this thesis is
based on the one described in [10], but is not identical. The growth-method implemented in this thesis
can, for example, handle multiple load cases as opposed to the one described in [10]. This is due to a
simultaneous topology and shape optimization utilization in this thesis, whereas a shape optimization
in which the cross-sectional areas are determined based on the stresses is used in [10]. Determining the
cross-sectional areas based on the stresses only holds for statically determinant structures, and when
multiple load cases are involved the structure will not be statically determinant. Other di’¼Ćerences
will also be discussed in this section. The fundamental structure of an optimization program utilizing
the growth-method can be seen in the ’¼éowchart in Figure 2.5 below.
Figure 2.5: Flowchart of optimization program utilizing the growth-method. Taken from [2].
The growth-method is written in the Matlab script called TrussOpt.m (see Appendix H.15). The
scripts Plotcheck0.m, PlotcheckB.m, and PlotcheckA.m (see Appendix H.16, H.17, and H.18) are
all used to plot the structure during each growth process. The script PlotcheckC.m (see Appendix
H.19) is used to plot the optimized structure after each growth process. Finally the script Plot.m
(see Appendix H.20) is used to display the ’¼ünal optimized structure, both undeformed and deformed.
A standard input-’¼üle for TrussOpt.m providing the nodal coordinates, and the load and boundary
conditions can be seen in Appendix H.21. In addition to the maximum iterations stopping criteria
shown in Figure 2.5 another stopping criteria is that the change in the objective function value is below
some small number, ╬Ą, de’¼üned by the user. If the growth-method reaches several hundred iterations
(several hundred new nodes) the volume will decrease very little, only the 5th-6th decimal point will
change. Therefore if ╬Ą is set to, for example, 10ŌłÆ4 then the algorithm will terminate even though the
maximum iteration criteria has not been reached yet.
In order to grow the structure, new nodes and elements need to be added. The growth-algorithm
utilizes two di’¼Ćerent ways of doing this. It either adds a new joint in truss members which intersect,
or it bisects a member adding a new node and introducing new truss members from all neighbouring
nodes to this new bisected node. The growth-algorithm is constructed so that it will always try to
add new nodes in intersecting members rather than bisecting a member. Hence before any growing is
initialized the program will loop over all existing elements and check for intersecting members. If no
Louis Christo’¼Ćersen (s102928)
==PAGE:31==
2.5.
Growth-method
13
intersections are found an element will be bisected. More details about the intersection and bisection
growing methods are found in the subsections 2.5.2 and 2.5.3.
Unless otherwise mentioned the growth-method will minimize the total volume with a constraint on
the compliance. The optimization problem can therefore be formulated as follows:
min

2.5.1
Intersecting members check
As mentioned earlier a check for intersecting members in the whole truss structure is carried out in
each iteration before any growing and optimizing of the structure takes place. In Figure 2.7 below two
intersecting lines and their nodal coordinates can be seen. The notation in this ’¼ügure will be used to
describe whether or not two lines intersect.
Figure 2.7: Intersecting truss elements in 2D. Taken from [2].
Using the notation from the ’¼ügure above and the standard formulation of a straight line (y = ax + b)
the equations for the two intersecting lines can be written as
Line 1:
y = y2 ŌłÆy1
x2 ŌłÆx1

Solving for x and y in the equation system above will yield one solution if the lines intersect. One
should keep in mind however, that all non-parallel lines will intersect somewhere in the in’¼ünite 2D-
plane, but the important thing to check is whether or not they intersect within the boundaries of the
two truss elements. The (x, y)-solution from the equation system above is therefore checked with the
following inequalities to ensure that the intersection lies within the boundaries of the two elements.
x > min (x1, x2)
,
x > min (x3, x4)
,
x < max (x1, x2)
,
x < max (x3, x4)
(2.20)
If all of these inequalities are true the intersection solution (x, y) lies within the boundaries of the two
elements.
2.5.2
Intersection
If one or more intersections are found in the intersection check, described in the previous subsection, a
new node will be added in the proximity of the intersection point. The exact location of the new node
is found by cubic polynomials (cubic splines) or from perturbation (described later in Section 2.5.4).
The position of the new node along with the de’¼ünition of the cubic splines and their perpendiculars
can be seen in Figure 2.8 below.
Louis Christo’¼Ćersen (s102928)
==PAGE:33==
2.5.
Growth-method
15
a Position of new node.
b Splines and perpendiculars de’¼ünition.
Figure 2.8: New node by intersection.
The perpendiculars, mj,1, mk,1, mj,2, and mk,2 de’¼ünes the tangent of the cubic spline at the endpoints
of each element.
The slope of the perpendiculars themselves are determined by the slopes of the
adjoining elements. Each of the four nodes (j1, k1, j2, and k2) in Figure 2.8 has at least one other
element connected to it besides the intersected element. The perpendicular will be de’¼üned as the
line which is perpendicular to a line which slope is calculated as the average of the slopes of all the
adjoining elements (excluding the intersected element). This de’¼ünition of the perpendiculars ensures
that the new node is placed in between the original element and the crossing of the perpendiculars,
which guarantees improved orthogonality for the neighbouring nodes. If an element is connected to a
boundary node the reaction force in this boundary node is also taken into account when calculating
the direction of the perpendicular. Now that the perpendiculars are de’¼üned, the exact shape of the
cubic polynomial can be found. The general expression of a cubic polynomial is given by
y(x) = ax3 + bx2 + cx + d
(2.21)
and the derivative is easily found to be
yŌĆ▓(x) = 3ax2 + 2bx + c
(2.22)
The problem is now to ’¼ünd the constants, a, b, c, and d. The cubic polynomial associated to element
one in Figure 2.8 needs to ful’¼ül the following four equations:
y(x1) = ax3
1 + bx2
1 + cx1 + d = y1
(2.23)
y(x2) = ax3
2 + bx2
2 + cx2 + d = y2
(2.24)
yŌĆ▓(x1) = 3ax2
1 + 2bx1 + c = mj,1
(2.25)
yŌĆ▓(x2) = 3ax2

(2.26)

With the four equations the four unknowns can be found and the shape of the cubic spline can be

The same procedure is performed for element two, and ’¼ünally the new node can be
introduced at the intersection between the two cubic splines. This intersection point is simply found
Louis Christo’¼Ćersen (s102928)
==PAGE:34==
2.5.
Growth-method
16
by equating the expressions for the two cubic splines and solving for x. The x-coordinate is then
inserted in either of the two expressions to determine the y-coordinate.
An extremely important
point when introducing new nodes by the intersection method is to check all intersections. If more
than one intersection occurs, they one at a time have a new node introduced to them utilizing the
procedure described above. After the new node has been added the truss structure is topology and
shape optimized (using the simultaneous method) and the value of the objective function is saved.
When the second intersection gets a new node added to it and the structure is optimized, the new
node from intersection one is not present (intersection one goes back to its original shape). When all
intersections with their new nodes have been optimized, the intersection which reduces the value of
the objective function the most, gets to keep its new node. The new structure is then saved into the
memory of the program and the growth-process continues.
2.5.3
Bisection
In the scenario where no intersections occur an element has to be bisected. The bisected member is
de’¼üned in Figure 2.9 below.
a Position of new node.
b Spline and perpendiculars de’¼ünition.
Figure 2.9: New node by bisection.
The perpendiculars mj and mk, and the expression of the cubic spline are de’¼üned in the same way
as for the intersection scenario. A third line, me, is de’¼üned as the line which is perpendicular to the
bisected element and also intersects the midpoint of the bisected element. As seen in the ’¼ügure the new
node is placed at the intersection between line me and the cubic spline. This ensures that the new node
is placed in between the original element and the crossing of the perpendiculars, mj and mk, which
guarantees improved orthogonality for the neighbouring nodes. When an element is bisected and the
new node is added all other nodes are connected to the new node. A topology and shape optimization
(simultaneous method) is then performed on this new structure and the objective function value is
saved. The same procedure is applied to all elements one at a time, and the structure which yields
the lowest objective function value is saved as the new grown structure.
With this method a lot
of new elements are added and therefore after the optimization process members with insigni’¼ücant
cross-sectional area should be removed before growing the structure anew.
2.5.4
Pertubation
An alternative way to determine the location of newly introduced nodes is by perturbation. The cubic
spline method described above can in rare cases cause the fmincon optimizer in Matlab to get stuck in
local minima. In addition the cubic spline method does not signi’¼ücantly improve the run-time, which
will be shown later on in the results chapter. The perturbation method is thus the safer and more
stable choice. When an element is bisected the location of the new node is simply given by
Louis Christo’¼Ćersen (s102928)
==PAGE:35==
2.5.
Growth-method
17
xnew = x2 + x1
2
+ pele
,
ynew = y2 + y1
2
+ pele
(2.28)
where (x1, y1) and (x2, y2) are the coordinates of the end nodes of the bisected element, and le is the
length of the bisected element. The perturbation factor, pe, is usually set to 0.01 unless otherwise
mentioned. In the case of an intersection the exact same perturbation formula is used, only le is chosen
randomly to be the length of either of the two intersecting elements.
2.5.5
Upper and lower bounds on design variables
To ensure that the optimization process executes smoothly some lower and upper bounds on the
design variables are de’¼üned. For the cross-sectional areas the lower bound is set to Amin = 10ŌłÆ10
and not exactly zero as this will result in a singular sti’¼Ćness matrix. The upper bound on the cross-
sectional area is set to Amax = 10. The bounds on the nodal coordinates are set by the Matlab
script localboundaries.m (see Appendix H.22), which assigns a rectangular domain to each node
within which it can move freely in the optimization process. The script takes other nodes into account
when creating the domains, such that no nodes can move into each others territories. New nodes can
be introduced basically anywhere though. In Figure 2.10 below some examples of these local nodal
boundary boxes can be seen. The plots are generated by the script Plotcheckbound.m (see Appendix
H.24).
a 1 free node.
b 5 free nodes.
Figure 2.10: Local boundary boxes for crane structure.
In some cases it may be advantageous to further restrict the bounds on the nodal movement. A factor
between 0 and 1 is introduced, such that 1 corresponds to the boundary boxes shown in Figure 2.10
and 0 corresponds to no nodal movement. The boundary boxes using a restriction factor of 0.8 for
the same example as shown above can be seen in Figure 2.11 below.
Louis Christo’¼Ćersen (s102928)
==PAGE:36==
2.5.
Growth-method
18
Figure 2.11: Local boundary boxes for 5 free nodes with a restriction factor of 0.8.
Unless otherwise mentioned, a restriction factor of 0.9 will be used throughout this thesis.
2.5.6
Optimizing run-time
As the truss structure grows the run-time of the optimization process obviously increases as more and
more elements have to undergo the intersection check. Additionally, if no intersections are present,
every single element has to be bisected and each corresponding structure has to be optimized. Some
measures can be taken in order to decrease the run-time of the optimization. In the following two
methods to decrease run-time is discussed; restrictions on adding new members and an ŌĆØoptimalŌĆØ
initial guess.
Restrictions on adding members
When using the growth-method it becomes very tedious to connect the new nodes in the bisected
members to every single node in the structure, especially when the number of nodes become large.
Therefore certain restrictions on how many members are added to these newly introduced nodes are
formulated. These restrictions will decrease the run-time of the following topology and shape opti-
mization as the structure grows. In this thesis two di’¼Ćerent restrictions on adding members will be
considered; the crossing restriction and the indeterminacy restriction. These two methods are pre-
sented in [10].
Crossing restriction: The crossing restriction simply states that members are only added from the
new node to other nodes if the members cross less than or equal to some number of other members,
nc, de’¼üned by the user. The value of nc should not be much larger than 2, otherwise the e’¼Ćect of this
restriction will not be great until very late in the growth process.
Indeterminacy restriction: It is known that a truss structure will be statically determinant if
optimized for volume with a compliance constraint and a single load case. According to Michell it is
known that optimal least-weight structures (same as minimizing volume) are comprised of members
crossing each other orthogonally (or close to orthogonal). This knowledge can be taken advantage of
when connecting members from a new node to the neighbouring nodes. The indeterminacy factor, I,
is de’¼üned by the user as an integer value. Only the I + 1 members which are closest to a 90 degree
angle to the bisected member are added. By de’¼üning the number of added members as I + 1, the
Louis Christo’¼Ćersen (s102928)
==PAGE:37==
2.6.
Three dimensions
19
value of I will represent the number of times that the grown structure (before any topology or shape
optimization is performed) is statically indeterminant. Notice that this restriction is not valid for
multiple load case structures as they will not necessarily be statically determinant.
ŌĆØOptimalŌĆØ initial guess
Instead of adding new members with some prescribed cross-sectional area, it would be better to assign
them a more optimal cross-sectional area to be used in the following optimization. It is known that for
statically determinant truss structures the most optimal cross-sectional areas, in terms of minimizing
the volume, are obtained when each element has the same contribution to the target compliance of
the structure. By target compliance is meant the maximum allowable compliance as de’¼üned by the
constraint set by the user. Thus the optimal compliance for each element, copt,e, is given by
copt,e = cmax
ne
(2.29)
where cmax is the maximum allowable compliance of the structure, and ne is the total number of
elements. The optimal cross-sectional area can now be found by
Aopt,e = cold,e
copt,e
Aold,e
(2.30)
where the subscript old, e refers to the initial values. Very importantly, it should be noted that this
only applies to statically determinant structures. When members are added from the new node to
neighbouring nodes the structure will be statically indeterminant. Utilizing the method described
above for an indeterminant structure will therefore not yield the optimal cross-sectional areas, hence
the quotation marks in the title of this section. Even though the cross-sectional areas will not be
optimal when the structure is indeterminant, they will still provide a better initial guess for the opti-
mization than some random values provided by the user.

2.6.1
Topology optimization
As mentioned above topology optimization in 3D is formulated exactly as the 2D-version. Therefore
this section solely serves the purpose of demonstrating a simple example of topology optimization in
3D. The Matlab scripts used for 3D topology optimization are called the same as the 2D versions,
only with a ŌĆØ3DŌĆØ in the end, i.e. TopOpt.m becomes TopOpt3D.m. All of the scripts can be seen in
Appendices H.25-H.29. The script plotting the initial and optimized structures is called truss3D.m
and can be seen in Appendix H.30. In Figure 2.14 below the compliance of a simple crane structure
is optimized.
a Loads and boundary conditions.
b Optimized structure, object compliance and constraint
1.0 x initial volume (az = 125, el = 10).
Figure 2.14: Topology optimization of 3D crane (blue is tension, red is compression, and green is zero stress).
Initially all elements have the same cross-sectional area. The optimizer redistributes the allowable
volume to the three outer members to minimize the compliance.
The three members connecting
the supports are deemed unimportant by the optimizer (they have zero stress) and can therefore be
removed in post-processing. The stresses in the truss structure are calculated by the Matlab-function
stressfunc3D.m (see Appendix H.31).
The performance of the optimization can be assessed by
Equation (2.8), and it is found that the initial compliance was reduced by 45.4 % in this example.
2.6.2
Shape optimization
This section again solely serves the purpose of demonstrating a simple example of shape optimization
in 3D. The Matlab script used for 3D shape optimization is called GeomOpt3D.m and can be seen in
Appendix H.32. In Figure 2.15 on the next page the compliance and volume of a simple cantilever is
optimized.
The only free nodes to be optimized are the two top nodes at the end of the cantilever.
When
optimizing the compliance (see Figures 2.15b and 2.15c) it is seen that these two nodes merge into the
same position. When optimizing the volume (see Figures 2.15d and 2.15e) the merging of the nodes is
not seen, instead they both move further backwards (closer to the supports) and towards the center
of the structure. Furthermore it is noticed that both of the optimized structures are symmetric. The
performance of the optimizations are again assessed by Equation 2.8, and it is found that the initial
compliance was reduced by 8.9 % and the initial volume was reduced by 17.2 %.

Firstly it is noticed that the combined optimization also creates a symmetric structure. The two free

2.6.4
Growth-method
The growth-method in 3D works in essentially the same way as the 2D model. New nodes are created
either by bisection of a member or by intersection of two members. The location of the new nodes
can again be determined either from the use of splines or from perturbation. It has been decided
to focus on perturbation and not splines when dealing with the growth-method in 3D. As will be
shown in the results chapter perturbation yields a similar run-time to using splines, and generally the
perturbation method is more robust. The main Matlab script used for the growth-method in 3D is
called TrussOpt3D.m and can be seen in Appendix H.38.
Checking for ŌĆØintersectionsŌĆØ
In two dimensions it is easy for two elements to intersect. In three dimensions however, it can easily
happen that the growth-method will create elements which do not exactly intersect but cross each
other closely. Therefore in order to make use of the feature of introducing new nodes by intersection,
di’¼Ćerent intersection rules than the ones used for two dimensions will have to be applied for three
dimensions. An ŌĆØintersectionŌĆØ in the 3D-model will occur whenever the shortest distance between any
two elements is lower than some threshold value set by the user. In other words an ŌĆØintersectionŌĆØ
occurs if the following inequality is true:
d(e1, e2) Ōēż╬▒lmax
(2.34)
where d(e1, e2) is the shortest distance between any two elements e1 and e2, ╬▒ is some positive value
set by the user, and lmax is the length of the longest element in the current truss structure. By scaling
the threshold with the length of the longest element the intersection criteria will get stricter as the
structure grows ’¼üner and more complex. If the intersection criteria stays constant as more and more
members are added, the number of intersections could start to increase rapidly. The scaling with the
longest element length tries to prevent this behaviour. For details on how to compute d(e1, e2), see
Appendix A.
The shortest distance is checked by the inequality in Equation (2.34) to see whether or not an inter-
section occurs. The function DistBetween2Segment.m (see Appendix H.39) taken from [12] is used in
the main Matlab script to determine the shortest distance between two elements. If an intersection
does occur, the location of the new node has to be determined. If the elements intersect precisely the
new node will simply be added at the intersection point. Otherwise the new node will be introduced
halfway along the vector connecting the shortest distance between the two elements. Lastly it should
be mentioned that if three or more elements intersect in the same point they will not be treated as
separate intersections in pairs of two elements. This kind of intersection will be treated as one big
intersection and a new node will simply be introduced in the given intersection point.
Louis Christo’¼Ćersen (s102928)
==PAGE:42==
2.6.
Three dimensions
24
Stability issues
Certain stability issues occurs when utilizing the growth method in 3D. There are two main problems;
removing low cross-sectional area members can sometimes lead to an unstable structure (singular
sti’¼Ćness matrix) and creating new nodes in an intersection will produce a mechanism.
The ’¼ürst problem is hard to deal with without implementing additional constraints on the optimization
problem. With the combined topology and shape optimization problem described so far it is hard to
know which low cross-sectional area members can be removed without creating an unstable structure.
Instead the eigenvalues of the structure can be incorporated into the optimization problem. A stable
structure is characterized by having all eigenvalues greater than zero. Therefore by constraining the
eigenvalues to be larger than zero the optimizer will not create these low cross-sectional area members
at locations in which they are necessary in order to keep the structure stable. The formulation of the
eigenvalue problem along with the implementation of eigenvalues as constraints in the optimization
problem is described in the next section (Section 2.8).
The second main problem occurs when new nodes are introduced in an intersection, as this will create
a mechanism. The mechanism occurs because the new node does not have any sti’¼Ćness in the direc-
tion perpendicular to the plane of intersection. The resulting mechanism can be prevented though, by
adding an extra member along with the new node. This extra member should be connected from the
new node to any other node which is not located in the same plane as the intersecting members. Each
possible location for this new member is optimized, and the location which minimizes the volume of
the structure the most will be chosen.
Alternatively every stability problem occurring in the 3D growth-method can be dealt with by utilizing
beam elements instead of bar elements. Beam elements are capable of carrying loads by bending and
they can transfer moments in their nodal connections. Such an element is therefore stable by itself,
and no extra constraints, as for the eigenvalue solution, would have to be implemented. A beam
element is obviously more complicated to implement as it has twice as many degrees of freedom. In
addition to the translational degrees of freedom the beam element has rotational degrees of freedom
about the x-, y-, and z-axis. Derivations of objective and constraint functions and their derivatives
using a beam element will be shown later on in Section 2.9.
Upper and lower bounds on design variables
The design variables in the 3D growth-method are again the cross-sectional areas and the nodal
coordinates of the free nodes (nodes that are neither loaded nor bounded). The lower and upper bound
values for the cross-sectional areas are the same as described for the 2D growth-method in section
2.5.5. The bounds on the nodal coordinates are set by the Matlab script localboundaries3D.m (see
Appendix H.40), which assigns a rectangular three-dimensional box to each node within which it can
move freely in the optimization process. The script takes other nodes into account when creating
the boxes, such that no nodes can move into each others territories.
In Figure 2.17 below some
examples of these local nodal boundary boxes can be seen. The plots are generated by the script
Plotcheckbound3D.m (see Appendix H.41).
The boundary boxes seen above have a restriction factor of 0.9, which means that no boxes are touching
each other along the edges.

2.7
Multiple load cases
As mentioned earlier the growth-method implemented in this thesis is capable of handling multiple
load cases. This is due to the fact that the cross-sectional areas are not determined from the stresses,
but are treated as design variables along with the nodal coordinates. The growth algorithm for single
load cases need therefore not be changed in order to handle multiple load cases, only the formulation
of the optimization problem is changed from Equation (2.17) to:
min
:
f = V (x) =
ne
X

where m is the speci’¼üc load case, and nl is the number of load cases. The only di’¼Ćerence from the
formulation for single load cases is that more constraints are present. There is still only one objective
function (the total volume of the structure), but now nl constraints instead of just one constraint.
Notice that cmax is also written with a subscript, m, as the di’¼Ćerent load cases may have di’¼Ćerent
maximum allowable compliances. Moreover it should be noted that the indeterminacy restriction on
adding members can no longer be used, as the theory behind this restriction is based on statically
determinant structures. Structures with multiple load cases are not necessarily statically determinant.
2.7.1
Merge nodes
When using the growth-method on certain structures it can be useful to merge two nodes into one,
and then of course merge the members connected to those two nodes as well. A merging occurs when
the length of any member in the structure is smaller than some user-de’¼üned merging length. If the

2.8
Constraints on free vibration
In some cases it can be advantageous or maybe even necessary to ensure that the lowest natural
frequency (fundamental frequency) of the optimized structure is higher than some threshold value.
If it is known that the structure will be exposed to vibrations in a certain frequency range, it can
be ensured in the optimization process through constraints that the optimized structure will have
natural frequencies outside of this range, in order to prevent resonance. Incorporating constraints on
the natural frequencies can furthermore be used to make certain that the optimized structure will not
engage in rigid body moments. Rigid body moments are characterized by a natural frequency of zero.
Thus forcing the optimization process to create an optimized structure with natural frequencies above
zero will result in a stable structure. The natural frequencies of a truss structure can be found by
solving the following eigenvalue problem:
 K ŌłÆŽē2M

ŽĢ = 0
(2.37)
where K is the sti’¼Ćness matrix, Žē is the natural frequency given in [rad/s], ŽĢ is the eigenvector, and
M is the mass matrix.

A simple two-dimensional example is studied to illustrate the e’¼Ćect of the eigenvalue constraint.
In Figure 2.18 on the next page a cantilever truss is topology optimized both without and with a
constraint on the lowest eigenvalue (volume is minimized and compliance constrained).
It is clearly seen that when the lowest eigenvalue of the structure is being forced higher the optimizer
increases the cross-sectional area of the bars closest to the supports. Moving mass closer to the sup-
ports will result in higher eigenvalues of the structure. It should also be noted that the eigenvalue
constraint costs a lot in terms of reducing the initial volume. All structures are optimized with the
same compliance constraint, and while the topology optimization without an eigenvalue constraint

The purpose of this eigenvalue constraint section is to introduce the basic theory for eigenvalue calcula-
tion and incorporation into an optimization constraint. This eigenvalue constraint will mainly be used
in the 3D growth-method. The 3D growth-method is plagued by stability issues when members are
removed by the optimizer. Including a constraint on the lowest eigenvalue in the 3D growth-method
optimizer will assist in ensuring stability of the structure. As the 3D growth-method utilizes com-
bined simultaneous topology and shape optimization the sensitivity of this new eigenvalue constraint
with respect to the nodal coordinates is also required (for the shape part of the optimization). The
derivation of this sensitivity can be seen in Appendix B.
2.9
Beam-element model
As mentioned earlier, utilizing a beam element in the 3D growth-method can e’¼Ćectively solve the
stability issues associated with removing low cross-sectional area members and introducing new nodes
in intersections. This is due to the fact that the beam element has rigid connections which allows for
transfer of moment and it is also able to sustain transverse loads through bending. In Figure 2.19
below a sketch de’¼üning the 3D beam element can be seen.
Louis Christo’¼Ćersen (s102928)

It can be seen that the beam element has twice as many degrees of freedom (upq) as the truss element
(see Figure 2.12). The additional degrees of freedom consist of the rotation about the x-, y-, and
z-direction. The Matlab scripts using the beam elements are not included in the appendix report, as
these scripts are identical to the truss ones, with the exception of the sti’¼Ćness matrix formulation, the
derivatives with respect to the nodal coordinates, and the indication of tension/compression. These
three topics are discussed below, hence there is no need to include the beam Matlab scripts in the
appendix report.

is taken from [14] and can be seen in Appendix C. In addition to YoungŌĆÖs modulus E (associated
with axial deformation) the matrix includes the moment of inertia related to bending around the
y-axis (Iy), the moment of inertia related to bending around the z-axis (Iz), and the shear modulus
G and torsional constant K (related to torsion about the x-axis). In this work only solid circular
cross-sections will be considered for the beam elements. For a solid circular cross-section the sti’¼Ćness
parameters can be written as

2.9.3
Indication of tension/compression
For the truss element the stress is used to indicate whether a member is in tension or compression and
hence whether said member should be blue or red in the plot of the optimized structure. When dealing
with beam elements however, it does not make sense to describe the stress state of the element using
just a single stress, as both axial, bending, and torsional sti’¼Ćness is present in a beam element. In
order to assign colors (red or blue) to an optimized structure using beam elements the stress associated
with the axial force in the beam element is used. The section forces at the two endpoints of an element
can be calculated as

The 6 section forces are: axial force, shear force in y-direction, shear force in z-direction, moment
in y-direction, moment in z-direction, and torsional moment. Notice that while the shear forces and
moments may vary along the beam element the axial force is constant. Therefore for the given purpose
it does not matter whether fe(1, 1) or fe(7, 1) is used, as long as the change of sign for fe(1, 1) is carried

2.10
Geometric constraints
By geometric constraint is meant a zone in the design domain in which elements are not allowed to
exist. In this thesis geometric constraints are implemented in 2D, but not 3D. In 2D the geometric
constraint can take any shape, although common zone shapes include the rectangle and the circle.
In this work a circular geometric constraint is studied. In practical applications structures often has
to allow space for ventilation or other obstacles. Geometric constraints are therefore implemented in
order to add more practical functionality to the growth-method program.
2.10.1
Circular shape
The fundamental idea when implementing the geometric constraint in the growth-method is to penalize
the volume of members which exists in the constraint zone. By arti’¼ücially increasing the volume of
members in the constraint zone the objective function will drastically increase and thereby, with the
help of gradient information, advise the optimizer to avoid letting elements cross over the constraint
zone. The objective function, the volume, is therefore rede’¼üned as
V =
ne
X
e=1
Aele + pfac
ne
X
e=1
Aelx
e
(2.61)
where pfac is a penalization factor chosen by the user, and lx
e is the cross-length of the element. The
second term in Equation (2.61) represents the arti’¼ücial volume. This term scales with the cross-length
and therefore the more an element violates the constraint zone the more it will increase the objective
function. Calculating the cross-length for a circular constraint can be split up into two scenarios. In
the ’¼ürst scenario the element penetrates the circle at one point and exits the circle at another point.
In the second scenario the element penetrates the circle, but does not exit the circle. Either way the
intersection(s) between each element and the circular constraint has to be found. This consists of
simply solving the following two equations for the two unknowns x and y:
Line:
y = y2 ŌłÆy1
x2 ŌłÆx1
x + y1 ŌłÆy2 ŌłÆy1
x2 ŌłÆx1
x1
(2.62)
Circle:
(x ŌłÆcx)2 + (y ŌłÆcy)2 = r2
(2.63)
where (cx, cy) is the center of the circle, and r is the radius. Due to the quadratic equation this system
of equations will produce two solutions for (x, y). If the two solutions are identical the element is
tangent to the circle and does therefore not violate the constraint (lx
e = 0). If both solutions are
imaginary the element does not intersect with the circle and does therefore not violate the constraint
(lx
e = 0). The last case in which both solutions are real require more inspection in order to determine
the nature of the violation. Firstly it should be checked if the solutions are even within the boundaries
of the element.
This check is performed as in Equation (2.20).
If both solutions are within the
Louis Christo’¼Ćersen (s102928)
==PAGE:51==
2.11.
ipOPT
33
boundaries of the element the cross-length is given by the length of the vector which connects the two
solutions. If only one solution is within the boundaries of the element it means that one of the elements
endpoints lies inside the circle. The cross-length in this case is found as the length of the vector which
connects this endpoint to the solution that is within the boundaries of the element. In the results
section (the next section) a structure subjected to a circular constraint will be optimized utilizing
the growth-method. Gradient information for the cross-length lx will be calculated numerically by
fmincon due to the complexity of the derivations. Additionally, the example illustrating the geometric
constraint is in 2D and is relatively simple and the numerically computed gradients are therefore not
very costly.
2.11
ipOPT
In this ’¼ünal section of the theory chapter an alternative optimizer is presented.
Instead of using
MatlabŌĆÖs build-in fmincon optimizer, an interface of ipOPT (Interior-Point Optimizer) can be used in
Matlab. The 3.10.3-version of ipOPT is utilized in this thesis. Relatively few modi’¼ücations are needed
in order to run the main 2D growth-method script with ipOPT instead of fmincon. See Appendix H.43
for the main 2D growth-method script using ipOPT. First, a few extra options need to be de’¼üned by
the user. These include; initialization of lagrangian multipliers, hessian approximation, an adaptive or
monotone mu strategy and a scaling method. Last, in addition to the gradient functions the structure
of the gradient vectors/matrices are also required to be user-de’¼üned when using ipOPT. In Appendix
E a simple example is run utilizing both fmincon and ipOPT and their performance is compared and
discussed.
Louis Christo’¼Ćersen (s102928)
==PAGE:52==

ipOPT
34
Louis Christo’¼Ćersen (s102928)

Chapter 3
Results & Discussion
The purpose of this chapter is to present the performance and features of the growth-method. Firstly
the 2D-model will be analysed through various examples including single load structures and multiple
load case structures. The e’¼Ćect on the performance of the algorithm by adjusting di’¼Ćerent features and
settings will be studied. Furthermore results from expanding the growth-method to three dimensions
will be presented.
3.1
2D Growth-method
Unless otherwise mentioned all of the examples in the 2D growth method section will represent a min-
imization of volume with the compliance constrained. The growth-method optimization is performed
by fmincon in Matlab. The objective function (the volume), the constraint (the compliance), and
their sensitivities are all normalized as this will usually improve the performance of fmincon. The
convergence settings used in these following examples, unless otherwise mentioned, are shown in Table
3.1 below.
Table 3.1: Convergence settings for fmincon in 2D growth-method examples.
Max iterations
Tolerance for x
Tolerance for obj. func.
Tolerance for constraint
2000
1e-7
1e-7
1e-6
Each optimization process terminates when the maximum iteration number is reached or when either
the relative change in design variables, x, or in objective function value is below the values shown
in the table above. The constraint tolerance is the maximum relative value with which the compli-
ance can exceed the maximum allowable compliance. The convergence settings used in [2] caused the
growth-method to break down relatively fast. Therefore more ŌĆØmax iterationsŌĆØ and smaller tolerances
are used in this work. Moreover an additional compliance check has been implemented. When fmin-
con outputs that the constraint (compliance) violation is zero it should be kept in mind that the low
cross-sectional area members are still present in this structure. Therefore an additional compliance
calculation is performed after removing low-area bars from the optimized structure. If this compliance
violates the constraint it means that removing the low-area bars has resulted in a mechanism, and the
current optimal structure will therefore not be chosen as the ’¼ünal grown structure. The cut-o’¼Ćlimit
for low area bars is set to 0.01 ├Ś max(Area) and the constraint is violated if the compliance exceeds
the maximum compliance by 1 % or more. This slack of 1 % is given in order to make the removal of
low area bars possible.
To speed up the run-time of the algorithm the for-loops are implemented in Matlab as a parfor-loops.
The ŌĆØparŌĆØ refers to parallel computing, and allows Matlab to distribute the optimization of the di’¼Ćer-
35
==PAGE:54==
3.1.
2D Growth-method
36
ent bisections and intersections to a speci’¼üed number of available cores. Notice that parallel computing
only works since the optimization of the di’¼Ćerent bisections and intersections are independent of each
other. In this chapter whenever a run-time is presented for a certain optimization, the number of cores
used will always be speci’¼üed alongside it.
Lastly it should be mentioned that in some of the plots it might look like the cross-sectional areas are
a little discontinuous. This is not the case though, as this issue has to do with some of the plots being
slightly pixelated.

It can be observed how the growth-algorithm systematically and symmetrically builds up the truss
structure. The fact that intersections have ’¼ürst priority over bisecting members can also clearly be
seen from the results. As soon as an intersection occurs a new node is added in the intersection point
and no members are bisected (see for example Figures 3.2c ŌåÆ3.2d and 3.2h ŌåÆ3.2i). Another trend
which can be observed is that the new bars which are alternately added from both sides always initi-
ate in the innermost space between the already existing bars. It can be di’¼ācult to tell from the line
thickness (representing the cross-sectional area) but the total volume of the structure (the objective
function) does indeed decrease with each new growth. In fact the initial volume is reduced by 27.5 %
after the addition of 80 new nodes (see Figure 3.2u). The reduction is evaluated using Equation (2.8).
The total run-time for this example was 519 minutes (using 12 cores).
Adding more and more nodes would further decrease the volume of the truss structure. The reduction
in volume from iteration to iteration decreases drastically as the structure grows though. In Figure
3.3 below the normalized volume along with the run-time is plotted as a function of added nodes.
Figure 3.3: Normalized volume and run-time as a function of new nodes added.
Notice that the volume is normalized by division with the initial volume. It can be seen how the
volume is reduced a lot as the ’¼ürst few nodes are added, and then as more and more nodes are
added the volume barely decreases. The run-time shows that after 40-50 nodes it becomes very time
expensive to add a new node. A more detailed run-time plot for the ’¼ürst 25 iterations can be seen
in Figure 3.4 below.
This plot also shows the average fmincon iterations, the number of calls to
the objective function gradient (sensobj), and the number of calls to the constraint (FEcon) and its
gradient (senscon).
Louis Christo’¼Ćersen (s102928)
==PAGE:57==
3.1.
2D Growth-method
39
Figure 3.4: Detailed run-time plot for the ’¼ürst 25 iterations.
The small plateaus on the run-time curve indicate when new nodes are added by intersection. As
discussed earlier intersection nodes are added a lot faster than bisection nodes. It can be seen that the
average number of fmincon iterations does not increase much. The number of objection/constraint
function calls increases almost exponentially though and in turn so does the run-time. The more
complicated the structure becomes the more calls the optimizer needs to make to the functions and
therefore at some point it becomes very expensive in time to add a new node.
By trial and error it has been found that a sum of two exponential functions ’¼üts the decrease in volume
the best. This ’¼ütted function is given by
Vnorm(n) = aebn + cedn
(3.1)
where Vnorm is the normalized volume and n is the number of new nodes added to the structure. Using
the build-in ’¼üt-function in Matlab the constants are found to a = 0.0276, b = ŌłÆ0.1237, c = 0.7273,
and d = ŌłÆ4.177 ┬Ę 10ŌłÆ5. Notice that the function has only been ’¼ütted to points 10-80 in Figure 3.3,
as this provides a better representation of the volume if further nodes are added (>80).
Fitting
a function to represent the decrease in normalized volume as a function of added nodes serves the
purpose of predicting the decrease in volume for n > 80. This prediction will obviously not be en-
tirely accurate, but it can serve as an initial indicator of the potential savings in volume by adding
additional nodes. It should be kept in mind that the ’¼üt goes towards zero for n ŌåÆŌł×which is ob-
viously not realistic. Therefore the ’¼üt is only valid for a limited number of extra nodes greater than 80.
Another interesting feature to notice about the optimized cantilever is its uncanny resemblance to the
ŌĆØMichell-cantileverŌĆØ. In Figure 3.5 below the least-weight truss, which is essentially the same as the
least-volume truss, for a cantilever with a point load can be seen.

3.1.2
Run-time
Recalling section 2.5 in the theory chapter there were di’¼Ćerent ways of determining the location of a
newly introduced node and di’¼Ćerent ways of reducing the amount of members added to a new node.
The location of a new node can either be determined from cubic splines or from perturbation. As
for reducing the amount of members added to a new node the indeterminacy restriction and/or the
crossing restriction can be enforced. In order to determine what con’¼üguration yields the lowest run-
time for the growth-method the short cantilever problem is studied. The short cantilever example
above is run using perturbation, an indeterminacy restriction of I = 2, and a crossing restriction of
nc = 1. In Table 3.2 on the next page the run-time of eight di’¼Ćerent test-runs of the short cantilever
example (9 nodes added) can be seen.
Louis Christo’¼Ćersen (s102928)
==PAGE:60==
3.1.
2D Growth-method
42
Table 3.2: Run-time analysis of 2D growth method optimization (short cantilever, 9 nodes added).
Test run
Location of new nodes
Indeterminacy restriction
Crossing restriction
Run-time (4 cores) [sec]
1

Not restricted
Not restricted
24.56
2
Cubic splines
Not restricted
Not restricted
25.36
3

25.05

24.03

Perturbation

25.80
8
Cubic splines
Not restricted
nc = 1
26.43
The reason for running only 9 iterations is due to the fact that the di’¼Ćerent restrictions causes the
algorithm to build up the structure di’¼Ćerently. For example if 18 iterations were run only 2 of those
18 new nodes would be intersection nodes when using restrictions, whereas using no restrictions would
create 6 of the 18 new nodes by intersection. As intersection nodes are generally added much faster
than bisection nodes this would be an unfair comparison.
From the table it can be seen that using perturbation with an indeterminacy restriction of I = 2 (test
run 5) yields the lowest run-time. The time saved in the optimization when starting out with a more
optimal node position from the cubic spline method is counterbalanced by the time it actually takes
to calculate this optimal node position. The starting position of the new node using the perturbation
method does not require any calculations, thus in the end the perturbation method and the cubic
spline method yields similar run-times. It can clearly be seen (comparing test run 5 and 6 to test
run 1 and 2) that enforcing an indeterminacy restriction on the amount of members added to new
nodes reduces the run-time. Additionally it is seen (comparing test run 7 and 8 to test run 1 and
2) that imposing a crossing restriction actually increases the run-time. This could have something
to do with the fact that a crossing restriction of nc = 1 will never allow more than one consecutive
intersection node to be added, whereas two or more of the cheaper intersection node additions can
occur in the other test runs. On the basis of this analysis it is decided to use perturbation with an
indeterminacy restriction of I = 2 from now on, unless otherwise mentioned, when using the growth-
method optimization algorithm. Notice that the indeterminacy restriction is not valid for multiple
load case structures, and will therefore not be used in those examples.

grow in every which direction, i.e. the unboundedness of the problem, allows the optimizer to minimize
the weight of the cantilever even further than point J.

3.1.5
Circular supported cantilever
In the examples shown so far the number of boundary nodes used is constant throughout the whole
growth-process.
In certain structures it can be an advantage to have several boundary nodes to
choose from during the optimization. The growth-method can easily handle this feature by de’¼üning
the desired amount of boundary nodes from the beginning, and then keeping all boundary nodes in
the structure throughout the whole growth-process even though they are not all utilized by the initial
topology optimization. The extra boundary nodes may then be taken into use in whichever iteration
the optimizer sees ’¼üt. A good example as to illustrate this feature is a simple cantilever supported
by a ring of boundary nodes (see Appendix H.44 for the Matlab input-’¼üle). In Figure 3.14 below the
initial structure (with 20 boundary nodes) can be seen along with the initial topology optimization.
a Initial structure.
b Initial topology optimization.
Figure 3.14: Initial structure and initial topology optimization.
This example is unbounded. Notice that only 2 of the 20 boundary nodes are utilized by the initial
topology optimization. If the other 18 boundary nodes are kept in the structure however, the growth-
method will make use of most of them at some point. This can be seen in Figure 3.15 below, where
selected optimized structures are shown for a growth-method optimization of 81 iterations.
Louis Christo’¼Ćersen (s102928)

==PAGE:69==
3.1.
2D Growth-method
51
The optimized structure seems logical and symmetrical (as expected due to the two load cases being
symmetric). It is worth noting that the inner structure resembles the optimized short cantilever for a
single load, and that the growth-method has then created an outer structure which ensures that each
of the two non-centrally located forces are transferred to the mid-span of the height to be carried by
the inner structure. The stresses shown by red and blue in the optimized structures above are for load
case F1. The stresses for load case F2 would simply be the opposite, i.e. every compression member
from F1 becomes a tension member in F2 and vice versa. This is due to the fact that the two load
cases are symmetrical and equal, and due to the fact that the boundary conditions are symmetrical.
Furthermore it can be seen that the growth-algorithm employs the node merging feature (described in
Section 2.7.1) in the bottom right corner of the structure going from 7 to 8 nodes (see Figures 3.18h
and 3.18i). Last, notice how triangular substructures, as opposed to the usual quadrilaterals, are
created at the tip of the cantilever (see Figure 3.18u). This is typical for multiple load case structures,
and will be discussed further in the next example, which is also a multiple load case example.
In this example the initial volume is reduced by 65.1 % and the run-time was recorded to 591 minutes
(12 cores). In Figure 3.19 below the normalized volume and the run-time is plotted as a function of
added nodes.
Figure 3.19: Normalized volume and run-time as a function of new nodes added.
The sum of two exponential functions (see Equation (3.1)) is found to serve as the best ’¼üt to the
decrease in volume.
The constants are found to a = 0.0371, b = ŌłÆ0.1608, c = 0.3511, and d =
ŌłÆ7.638┬Ę10ŌłÆ5. The function is ’¼ütted to iterations 10-77, in order to realistically predict the normalized

It is clearly seen that as for the 2D growth method the 3D growth method also builds up the optimized
structure in a systematic way. Another triangular sti’¼Ćener is connected to the upper tension chord
in each iteration. Also notice that the optimized structure is symmetric. In this example the initial
volume is reduced by 15.8 % and the run-time is 6 minutes (4 cores). It should also be mentioned
that this optimization is performed without any constraints on the eigenvalues of the structure. One
would expect this optimized tripod cantilever to resemble the 2D growth-method optimized cantilever
structures to a greater extend, i.e. also including tension members from the upper support crossing the
compression members perpendicularly. Optimized structures including these tension members do in
fact exist, and they even yield a lower volume than the optimized structures seen in Figure 3.24 above.
The 3D growth-algorithm does not choose them as optimal solutions though, as the compliance of
these structures do not obey the compliance constraint. They do not obey the compliance constraint
because they are not stable, i.e. the global sti’¼Ćness matrix is extremely close to being singular. In the
next subsection the same tripod cantilever is optimized using a 3D growth-algorithm which takes into
account a constraint on the eigenvalues of the optimized structures. This eigenvalue constraint should
prevent the stability issues, and the optimized structure should therefore resemble the 2D version more
Louis Christo’¼Ćersen (s102928)

closely.

Louis Christo’¼Ćersen (s102928)

optimizer adds a lot of small cross-sectional area members to support the main structure. The cross-
sectional area of these supporting members is approximately a factor 10 lower than the cross-sectional
area of the main structure members. If the supporting members are removed the main structure is
much easier visualized. In Figures 3.25m-3.25o the optimized structure, not including the supporting
members, can be seen in three di’¼Ćerent views. It can be seen that the constraint on the eigenvalues
takes care of the stability issues associated with adding the tension members. The optimized structure
now resemble the 2D optimal solution with crossing compression and tension members. In fact the 3D
optimal solution is simply two plane (2D) optimal solutions put together. In this example the initial
volume is reduced by 41.9 % and the run-time is recorded to 9 minutes (4 cores).
It should be mentioned that the supporting members are included in the volume calculation of the
optimized structures. Notice how the decrease in volume is much greater than for the previous ex-
ample without the eigenvalue constraint (41.9 % compared to only 15.8 %). As mentioned earlier,
when including the eigenvalue constraint the optimized structure corresponds to what would be ex-
pected from the 2D cantilever optimized structure. In other words the eigenvalue constraint allows the
growth-method optimizer to construct the ŌĆØcorrectŌĆØ solution, which explains the large di’¼Ćerence in
volume reduction from the previous example without an eigenvalue constraint to this example which
includes an eigenvalue constraint.

It is clearly seen that the same basic design is found. Most noticeable however is the cleanliness of the
individual optimized structures, i.e. no (or very few) supporting members are present. As mentioned
